1.5 m high-gain antenna |\n| **Power** | 1,800 W at 1 AU (solar panels) |\n| **Instruments** | MSI (Multi-Spectral Imager), NIS (Near-IR Spectrograph, 0.8-2.6 um), NLR (Laser Rangefinder), XGRS (X-ray/Gamma-Ray Spectrometer with BGO shield), MAG (Magnetometer, DC-10 Hz). 56 kg total, 80 W |\n| **Mathilde Flyby** | 1997-06-27, 1,212 km closest approach; 500+ images, 60% of surface |\n| **Eros Orbit Insertion** | 2000-02-14 15:33 UTC |\n| **Eros Orbit Parameters** | Initial: 321 x 366 km elliptical; final: 35 km circular polar retrograde; 230 orbits completed |\n| **Landing on Eros** | 2001-02-12 ~20:01 UTC, 1.5-1.8 m/s touchdown speed |\n| **Last Contact** | 2001-02-28 |\n| **Mission Duration** | 5 years, 21 days |\n| **Total Cost** | $224 million ($124.9M development, $44.6M launch, $54.6M operations) |\n| **Key Discoveries** | Eros is a primitive, undifferentiated body with uniform composition; surface regolith; high density of boulders; no magnetic field detected; post-landing gamma-ray spectrometer sensitivity increased 10x |\n| **Notable Firsts** | First spacecraft to orbit an asteroid; first spacecraft to land on an asteroid |\n| **Program** | Discovery Program |\n\n---\n\n### 1.129 Mars Global Surveyor\n\n| Field | Value |\n|-------|-------|\n| **Full Name** | Mars Global Surveyor (MGS) |\n| **Launch Date/Time** | 1996-11-07 17:00 UTC |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Delta II 7925 (D-239) |\n| **Launch Site** | Cape Canaveral AFS, LC-17A |\n| **Spacecraft Mass** | 1,030.5 kg (dry) |\n| **Power** | 980 W |\n| **Cost** | $154M development + $65M launch + ~$20M/year operations |\n| **Mars Arrival** | 1997-09-11 01:17 UTC |\n| **Orbit** | Sun-synchronous near-polar circular: 372.8 km periapsis, 436.5 km apoapsis, 92.9 deg inclination, 1.95 hr period |\n| **Mission End** | 2006-11-02 (loss of contact); declared ended 2007-01-28. Cause: software parameter update flaw |\n| **Duration** | 9 years, 11 months, 26 days |\n| **Instruments** | MOC (Mars Orbiter Camera: narrow-angle 1.5-12 m/pixel, wide-angle 240 m/pixel, daily global 7.5 km/pixel), MOLA (Mars Orbiter Laser Altimeter), TES (Thermal Emission Spectrometer), MAG/ER (Magnetometer/Electron Reflectometer), USO/RS (Ultrastable Oscillator), MR (Mars Relay) |\n| **Images Returned** | 240,000+ images spanning 4.8 Martian years |\n| **Key Discoveries** | Hundreds of gullies suggesting recent liquid water; layered crust >10 km deep; localized magnetic fields (no global field); hematite deposits; \"Swiss cheese\" south polar terrain; dust devil tracks; evidence of water flow between 1999-2001 |\n| **Notable Firsts** | First successful Mars mission in two decades; returned more data than all prior Mars missions combined; pioneered aerobraking at Mars |\n\n---\n\n### 1.130 Mars Pathfinder / Sojourner\n\n| Field | Value |\n|-------|-------|\n| **Full Name** | Mars Pathfinder / Sojourner Rover |\n| **Launch Date/Time** | 1996-12-04 06:58:07 UTC |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Delta II 7925 (D240) |\n| **Launch Site** | Cape Canaveral AFS, SLC-17 |\n| **Total Launch Mass** | 890 kg |\n| **Rover Mass** | 10.6 kg; 65 x 48 x 30 cm |\n| **Landing Date/Time** | 1997-07-04 16:56:55 UTC |\n| **Landing Site** | Ares Vallis, Chryse Planitia (19.098 N, 33.254 W) |\n| **Last Contact** | 1997-09-27 10:23 UTC |\n| **Mission Terminated** | 1998-03-10 |\n| **Cost** | $280 million (including launch and operations) |\n| **Lander Instruments** | IMP (Imager for Mars Pathfinder, stereoscopic with filters), ASI/MET (Atmospheric Structure/Meteorology Package) |\n| **Rover Instruments** | APXS (Alpha Proton X-ray Spectrometer), 3 CCD cameras (2 mono navigation, 1 color), laser stripe hazard detection |\n| **Data Volume** | 2.3 billion bits (287.5 MB) |\n| **Images** | 16,500 from lander; 550 from rover |\n| **Atmospheric Readings** | 8.5 million |\n| **Rover Distance** | ~100 m total; never >12 m from lander; 83 sols operational |\n| **Landing System** | Airbag: inflated at 355 m altitude; 14 m/s impact, 18G deceleration; first bounce 15.7 m high |\n| **Key Discoveries** | Andesitic rocks (past volcanic remelting); basaltic rocks from ancient floods; atmospheric dust ~1 um radius; evidence of warmer, wetter past |\n| **Notable Firsts** | First Mars rover; first airbag landing on another planet |\n\n---\n\n### 1.131 ACE (Advanced Composition Explorer)\n\n| Field | Value |\n|-------|-------|\n| **Full Name** | Advanced Composition Explorer (Explorer 71) |\n| **Launch Date/Time** | 1997-08-25 UTC |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Delta II 7920-8 |\n| **Launch Site** | Cape Canaveral AFS, SLC-17A |\n| **Spacecraft Mass** | 785 kg |\n| **L1 Arrival** | 1997-12-12 |\n| **Orbit** | Lissajous orbit around Sun-Earth L1, ~1.5 million km from Earth |\n| **Cost** | $106.8 million (under allocated $141.1M) |\n| **Instruments (9)** | SWICS (Solar Wind Ion Charge Spectrometer), SWIMS (Solar Wind Ion Mass Spectrometer), SEPICA (Solar Energetic Particle Ionic Charge Analyzer), ULEIS (Ultra Low Energy Isotope Spectrometer), SIS (Solar Isotope Spectrometer), CRIS (Cosmic Ray Isotope Spectrometer), EPAM (Electron Proton Alpha Monitor), MAG (Magnetometer), SWEPAM (Solar Wind Electron Proton Alpha Monitor) |\n| **Key Application** | NOAA Space Weather Prediction Center uses real-time ACE data; provides up to 1 hour advance warning of damaging solar storms |\n| **Collecting Power** | 10-10,000x greater than any previous mission |\n| **Notable** | Still operational as of 2026; longest-serving L1 space weather monitor |\n\n---\n\n### 1.132 Cassini-Huygens\n\n| Field | Value |\n|-------|-------|\n| **Full Name** | Cassini-Huygens (Saturn Orbiter / Titan Probe) |\n| **Launch Date/Time** | 1997-10-15 08:43:00 UTC |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Titan IV(401)B / Centaur-T B-33 |\n| **Launch Site** | Cape Canaveral AFS, SLC-40 |\n| **Orbiter Mass** | 2,150 kg |\n| **Probe Mass** | 350 kg |\n| **Total Launch Mass** | 5,712 kg (including 3,132 kg propellant) |\n| **Dimensions** | 6.8 m high x 4 m wide |\n| **Power** | RTG: 32.7 kg plutonium dioxide (28.3 kg pure Pu) |\n| **Total Cost** | ~$3.26 billion (NASA $2.6B, ESA $500M, ASI $160M) |\n| **Saturn Orbit Insertion** | 2004-07-01 |\n| **Huygens Titan Landing** | 2005-01-14 |\n| **Mission End** | 2017-09-15 (intentional atmospheric plunge) |\n| **Duration** | 19 years, 335 days total; 13 years, 76 days at Saturn |\n| **Total Orbits** | 294 Saturn orbits |\n| **Orbiter Instruments (12)** | ISS (Imaging Science Subsystem), RADAR, RSS (Radio Science), INMS (Ion/Neutral Mass Spectrometer), VIMS (Visible/Infrared Mapping Spectrometer), CIRS (Composite Infrared Spectrometer), CDA (Cosmic Dust Analyzer), RPWS (Radio/Plasma Wave Spectrometer), CAPS (Plasma Spectrometer), UVIS (Ultraviolet Imaging Spectrograph), MIMI (Magnetospheric Imaging Instrument), MAG (Dual-technique Magnetometer) |\n| **Huygens Instruments (6)** | HASI (Atmosphere Structure Instrument), GCMS (Gas Chromatograph Mass Spectrometer), ACP (Aerosol Collector/Pyrolyser), DISR (Descent Imager/Spectral Radiometer), DWE (Doppler Wind Experiment), SSP (Surface Science Package) |\n| **Gravity Assists** | Venus (2x), Earth (1x), Jupiter (1x) |\n| **Grand Finale** | 22 orbits between Saturn and rings (April 26 - September 15, 2017); ~1,200-mile gap; altitude 1,600-4,000 km above clouds |\n| **Enceladus Plumes** | ~98% water vapor, ~1% hydrogen, plus CO2, CH4, NH3, salts, simple organics, complex macromolecular organics (>200u); molecular hydrogen indicates hydrothermal processes analogous to Earth seafloor vents |\n| **Titan Lakes** | Kraken Mare (size of Caspian Sea + Lake Superior), Ligeia Mare (126,000 km2, ~500 km across), Punga Mare (~380 km across); methane-ethane composition with latitudinal variation |\n| **Ring Discoveries** | Propeller structures (thousands, up to thousands of miles long); moonlets ~0.5 mile diameter; spokes (electrostatic); rings relatively young (10-100 million years); Enceladus source of E ring |\n| **Notable Firsts** | First Titan landing; first Saturn orbiter; 7 new moons discovered; first landing on outer solar system body |\n\n---\n\n### 1.133 Lunar Prospector\n\n| Field | Value |\n|-------|-------|\n| **Full Name** | Lunar Prospector |\n| **Launch Date/Time** | 1998-01-07 02:28 UTC (Jan 6 EST) |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Athena II (four-stage) |\n| **Launch Site** | Cape Canaveral AFS, SLC-46 |\n| **Spacecraft Mass** | 295 kg |\n| **Orbit** | 100 km polar orbit around Moon |\n| **Mission End** | 1999-07-31 09:52:02 UTC (deliberate impact into Shoemaker crater) |\n| **Duration** | ~18 months |\n| **Cost** | $62.8 million |\n| **Instruments (6)** | Gamma-Ray Spectrometer, Alpha Particle Spectrometer, Neutron Spectrometer, Magnetometer, Electron Reflectometer, Doppler Gravity Experiment |\n| **Key Discoveries** | Water ice at both lunar poles (~330 million metric tons per pole mixed in regolith); strong localized magnetic fields; global rock-type distribution; 370-mile (600 km) diameter iron-rich core |\n| **Impact Speed** | 3,800 mph (6,100 km/h) into Shoemaker crater |\n| **Program** | Discovery Program (#3) |\n| **Notable** | No water plume detected from deliberate impact |\n\n---\n\n### 1.134 Deep Space 1\n\n| Field | Value |\n|-------|-------|\n| **Full Name** | Deep Space 1 (DS1) |\n| **Launch Date/Time** | 1998-10-24 UTC |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Delta II 7326 |\n| **Launch Site** | Cape Canaveral AFS, SLC-17A |\n| **Spacecraft Mass** | 486 kg gross (31.1 kg hydrazine + 81.5 kg xenon) |\n| **Cost** | $152.3 million ($94.8M development, $43.5M launch, $10.3M operations, $3.7M science) |\n| **NSTAR Ion Engine** | 30 cm xenon ion thruster; 92 mN thrust at 2,100 W; specific impulse 1,000-3,000 s; operated 16,265 hours; total delta-v ~4.8 km/s (largest by any spacecraft on own propulsion) |\n| **Xenon Propellant** | 82 kg |\n| **Asteroid Braille Flyby** | 1999-07-29 (pointing error limited high-res imaging) |\n| **Comet Borrelly Flyby** | 2001-09-22, 2,200 km closest approach; excellent images of 8x4 km nucleus |\n| **Instruments** | B&W camera, IR spectrometer, ion/electron detectors, magnetometer/plasma wave instrument |\n| **Technologies Tested** | 12 total, all successful: NSTAR ion engine, autonomous navigation (AutoNav), solar concentrator arrays, small deep-space transponder, and others |\n| **Notable Firsts** | First interplanetary spacecraft to use ion propulsion |\n| **Program** | New Millennium Program |\n\n---\n\n### 1.135 ISS Zarya Module (FGB)\n\n| Field | Value |\n|-------|-------|\n| **Full Name** | Zarya (Functional Cargo Block / FGB) |\n| **Launch Date/Time** | 1998-11-20 06:40 UTC |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Proton-K |\n| **Launch Site** | Baikonur Cosmodrome, Site 81, Kazakhstan |\n| **Mass** | 19,323 kg (42,600 lb) |\n| **Dimensions** | 12.56 m long x 4.11 m wide |\n| **Cost** | $220 million (paid by USA; vs. $450M for rejected Lockheed \"Bus-1\") |\n| **Builder** | Khrunichev State Research and Production Space Center (Moscow) under Boeing/NASA subcontract |\n| **Role** | First ISS module; initial power, propulsion, and guidance |\n| **Notable** | Built by Russia, financed by United States; foundation of ISS |\n\n---\n\n### 1.136 Mars Climate Orbiter\n\n| Field | Value |\n|-------|-------|\n| **Full Name** | Mars Climate Orbiter (MCO) |\n| **Launch Date/Time** | 1998-12-11 18:45:51 UTC |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Delta II 7425 |\n| **Launch Site** | Cape Canaveral AFS, SLC-17A |\n| **Spacecraft Mass** | 638 kg |\n| **Cost** | ~$327.6 million (Mars Surveyor '98 program: $193.1M development, $91.7M launch, $42.8M operations) |\n| **Loss Date** | 1999-09-23 (last contact) |\n| **Instruments** | MARCI (Mars Color Imager, two cameras, up to 1 km resolution), PMIRR (Pressure Modulated Infrared Radiometer, thermal IR + visible) |\n| **Propulsion** | LEROS 1B main engine (640 N); 4x 22 N thrusters (trajectory); 4x 0.9 N thrusters (attitude); hydrazine monopropellant |\n| **Failure Mode** | Navigation error: Lockheed Martin software output thrust in pound-force; JPL navigation assumed metric (newtons). Over 9-month journey, small errors accumulated. Spacecraft entered Mars atmosphere at ~57 km altitude (planned: ~150 km), 170 km lower than intended. Burned up. |\n| **Root Cause** | \"Small Forces\" ground software file used imperial units; trajectory model expected metric. Angular Momentum Desaturation (AMD) events compounded the error |\n| **Objectives** | Study Martian climate, atmosphere, surface changes; serve as Mars Polar Lander relay |\n| **Notable** | Most famous unit conversion error in aerospace history |\n\n---\n\n### 1.137 Mars Polar Lander\n\n| Field | Value |\n|-------|-------|\n| **Full Name** | Mars Polar Lander (MPL) |\n| **Launch Date/Time** | 1999-01-03 20:21:10 UTC |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Delta II 7425 |\n| **Launch Site** | Cape Canaveral AFS, SLC-17B |\n| **Spacecraft Mass** | 290 kg (lander) |\n| **Cost** | $165 million ($110M development, $45M launch, $10M operations) |\n| **Loss Date** | 1999-12-03 14:39 UTC (last telemetry, prior to EDL) |\n| **Target** | Planum Australe, near south pole of Mars |\n| **Instruments** | Robotic arm for soil digging, microphones for soil moisture, Mars Descent Imager (MARDI), LIDAR, meteorological instruments |\n| **Carried** | Two Deep Space 2 microprobes (penetrators, to bury 100 km from lander) |\n| **Failure Mode** | Software error: vibrations from leg deployment falsely identified as surface touchdown; descent engines shut down ~40 m above surface. Lander crashed. |\n| **Root Cause** | Inadequate testing; software not tested with leg deployment vibration; inadequate funding and poor management cited |\n| **Notable** | Both DS2 microprobes also failed (no contact after separation) |\n\n---\n\n### 1.138 Stardust\n\n| Field | Value |\n|-------|-------|\n| **Full Name** | Stardust / Stardust NExT |\n| **Launch Date/Time** | 1999-02-07 UTC |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Delta II 7426 |\n| **Launch Site** | Cape Canaveral AFS, SLC-17A |\n| **Spacecraft Mass** | 254 kg spacecraft + 45.7 kg sample return capsule = ~300 kg |\n| **Cost** | ~$199.6-212 million |\n| **Wild 2 Encounter** | 2004-01-02 19:22 UTC, 250 km (155 miles) closest approach |\n| **Sample Return** | 2006-01-15, capsule landed at Utah Test & Training Range |\n| **Collection Method** | Aerogel: 90 blocks of microporous silica in tennis-racket-sized tray, >1,000 cm2 collection area |\n| **Instruments** | CIDA (time-of-flight mass spectrometer), DFMI (dust flux monitor, PVDF), Navigation Camera (CCD), X-band transponder (Doppler/mass estimation) |\n| **NExT Extended Mission** | Tempel 1 flyby 2011-02-15, 181 km range, 72 images of nucleus; confirmed 150 m crater from Deep Impact |\n| **Key Discoveries** | First comet dust sample return; found minerals formed at high temperature (crystalline silicates) mixed with interstellar grains; amino acid glycine detected |\n| **Notable Firsts** | First sample return from a comet; first sample return beyond the Moon |\n| **Program** | Discovery Program |\n\n---\n\n### 1.139 Chandra X-ray Observatory\n\n| Field | Value |\n|-------|-------|\n| **Full Name** | Chandra X-ray Observatory |\n| **Launch Date/Time** | 1999-07-23 04:31 UTC |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Space Shuttle Columbia (STS-93) + IUS |\n| **Launch Site** | Kennedy Space Center, LC-39B |\n| **Spacecraft Mass** | 4,790 kg (dry);
